
==== Front
BiometrikaBiometrikabiometbiometBiometrika0006-34441464-3510Oxford University Press 10.1093/biomet/asw004asw004ArticlesCalibrated propensity score method for survey nonresponse in cluster sampling Kim Jae Kwang Department of Statistics, Iowa State University, Ames, Iowa 50011, U.S.A.Kwon Yongchan Paik Myunghee Cho Department of Statistics, Seoul National University, Seoul 151-742, Korea, ykwon0407@snu.ac.kr   myungheechopaik@snu.ac.krjkim@iastate.edu6 2016 17 3 2016 103 2 461 473 2 2015 12 2015 © 2016 Biometrika Trust2016Weighting adjustment is commonly used in survey sampling to correct for unit nonresponse. In cluster sampling, the missingness indicators are often correlated within clusters and the response mechanism is subject to cluster-specific nonignorable missingness. Based on a parametric working model for the response mechanism that incorporates cluster-specific nonignorable missingness, we propose a method of weighting adjustment. We provide a consistent estimator of the mean or totals in cases where the study variable follows a generalized linear mixed-effects model. The proposed method is robust in the sense that the consistency of the estimator does not require correct specification of the functional forms of the response and outcome models. A consistent variance estimator based on Taylor linearization is also proposed. Numerical results, including a simulation and a real-data application, are presented.

Calibration estimationNonignorable missingnessSurvey samplingWeighting
==== Body
1. Introduction
Nonresponse is frequently encountered in survey sampling, and ignoring it could lead to bias or loss of efficiency in estimation. Suppose that the outcome of interest is not fully observed but we have completely observed auxiliary variables. If the response indicator is independent of the outcome given the auxiliary variables, the data are missing at random (Rubin, 1976). When units are independent and the data are missing at random, several methods have been used to correct for unit nonresponse. Inverse probability weighting and imputation are two main methods for handling nonresponse, but they require assumptions for auxiliary models, namely the probability of observation in the former and the prediction model in the latter. Validity of the methods depends on correct specification of these auxiliary models. Many authors have investigated doubly robust methods using both models, but their approaches still require correct specification of one of them (Robins et al., 1994; Bang & Robins, 2005; Tan, 2006; Kang & Schafer, 2007; Cao et al., 2009; Kim & Haziza, 2014).

When survey outcomes are correlated, analysis with missing data becomes complicated. Furthermore, the nonresponse varies between clusters and may be related to a cluster-specific variable that is not always observable. For example, in the Planning Database of the 2010 U.S. census and the 2008–2012 American Community Survey presented in the Supplementary Material, various demographic and socioeconomic variables are provided at tract levels nested in county levels. Restricting our attention to the state of California, the number of census tracts per county ranges from one to 2346. In this dataset the variable representing aggregate housing unit value in dollars is missing for 6\documentclass[12pt]{minimal}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\usepackage{wasysym} 
\usepackage{amsfonts} 
\usepackage{amssymb} 
\usepackage{amsbsy}
\usepackage{upgreek}
\usepackage{mathrsfs}
\setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt}
\begin{document}
}{}$\cdot $\end{document}3% of the tracts, and the missing proportions across the county levels range from 0% to 17%, suggesting heterogeneity of missingness across the cluster levels. In Yuan & Little (2007), data that are cluster-specific are referred to as nonignorable when the response indicator depends on the cluster effects but is independent of the outcome given covariates and cluster effects. Under cluster-specific nonignorable missingness, it is not clear how to handle the unknown random cluster effect. Inserting the random effect predictor into the propensity score yields a biased estimator under cluster-specific nonignorable missingness. Under this missingness, Shao (2007) proposed a simple weighting adjustment method when no other auxiliary variable is present. Skinner & D'Arrigo (2011) proposed a new form of inverse probability weighting estimator that eliminates random effects by conditioning on a sum of the response indicators in a cluster. Although the conditional estimator is an excellent choice for small cluster sizes, the computation is not practically feasible when small and large clusters are mixed, as in the 2014 Planning Database.

Based on a parametric model for the response mechanism that incorporates cluster-specific nonignorable missingness, we propose a propensity score method under cluster sampling in cases where small and large cluster sizes are mixed. Under certain conditions, the proposed method provides a consistent estimator of the mean or totals when the study variable follows a generalized linear mixed-effects model. The main strategy is to restrict certain observed and expected quantities to be the same at each cluster level. The proposed estimator has the form of both a propensity score estimator and a doubly robust estimator, and it is robust in the sense that correctness of the functional form of either model is not required provided the outcomes are conditionally independent given the cluster-specific random effects. A consistent variance estimator based on Taylor linearization is also proposed. Computations for the proposed method are relatively easy, and the method can be applied directly to survey sampling.

In the classical survey sampling set-up, the clusters are often selected with unequal selection probabilities. In this case, the final sample can be regarded as a realization from two-stage cluster sampling, where the first stage takes a probability-proportional-to-size sample of size \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\usepackage{wasysym} 
\usepackage{amsfonts} 
\usepackage{amssymb} 
\usepackage{amsbsy}
\usepackage{upgreek}
\usepackage{mathrsfs}
\setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt}
\begin{document}
}{}$K$\end{document}, with the measure of size equal to the cluster size \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\usepackage{wasysym} 
\usepackage{amsfonts} 
\usepackage{amssymb} 
\usepackage{amsbsy}
\usepackage{upgreek}
\usepackage{mathrsfs}
\setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt}
\begin{document}
}{}$N_i$\end{document}, and the second stage is simple random sampling within each sampled cluster. Combining the two stages, the sampling weights are computed as \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\usepackage{wasysym} 
\usepackage{amsfonts} 
\usepackage{amssymb} 
\usepackage{amsbsy}
\usepackage{upgreek}
\usepackage{mathrsfs}
\setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt}
\begin{document}
}{}$w_{ij} \propto N_i^{-1}\times (N_i/n_i).$\end{document} If \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}
\usepackage{amsmath}
\usepackage{wasysym} 
\usepackage{amsfonts} 
\usepackage{amssymb} 
\usepackage{amsbsy}
\usepackage{upgreek}
\usepackage{mathrsfs}
\setlength{\oddsidemargin}{-69pt}
\begin{document}
}{}$n_i = m$\end{document}, then the sampling weights are all equal. Such self-weighting designs are very popular, particularly in household surveys. In other cases, we may use the sampling weights in a propensity score estimator. To highlight the main strategy, we present in §3 the proposed method without considering the unequal selection probabilities among clusters. In §4 we present asymptotic properties incorporating unequal selection probabilities.

3. Proposed method
3.1. Linear mixed-effect models
To motivate our proposal, assume for now that a predicted value of \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}

Constraint (7) means that the propensity score estimator applied to \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}

Calibration is also used for nonresponse adjustment in survey sampling (Kott, 2006; Chang & Kott, 2008). Constraint (8) is a special condition for handling cluster-specific nonignorable missingness. The weighting method for the case \documentclass[12pt]{minimal}

The following theorem asserts the asymptotic normality of the propensity score estimator in (12). A sketch of the proof is given in the Appendix.
Theorem 1.
Let the sequence of finite populations and samples be as described above. Suppose that the finite populations have likelihood as in (5) and that the samples satisfy (15) and (16). Assume that the response mechanism satisfies cluster-specific nonignorable missingness, as in (3). Then the propensity score estimator in (12) computed from (13) and (14) satisfies

Although the simulation results confirm that the conditional maximum likelihood method and the proposed method are both consistent, the computational time for the former is much longer.

6. Discussion
The proposed method is computationally attractive compared with that of Skinner & D'Arrigo (2011), and is robust in the sense that it does not require correct specification of the functional forms of the response and imputation models. It can be extended to the case where multiple covariates describe the response probability. When the number of covariates predicting the outcome model exceeds the number of fixed parameters in the response model, the overparameterization can be handled using the generalized method of moments. Causal inference with clustered data (Hudgens & Halloran, 2008; Brumback & He, 2011) is another direction of potential extension.

Supplementary material
Supplementary material available at Biometrika online includes an application of the proposed method to the Planning Database of the U.S. Census Bureau.

Acknowledgment
We thank two referees for very helpful comments. This research was supported by the U.S. National Science Foundation and the National Research Foundation of Korea.
